class=\"tabs\"><li class=\"selected\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a class=\"selected\" id=\"about-tab\" href=\"/faculty/directory/10992/Zittrain/\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"icon icon-user-tie\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"tab-label\">About</span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t  </li><li>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a id=\"publications-tab\" href=\"/faculty/directory/10992/Zittrain/publications\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"icon icon-pen\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"tab-label\">Publications</span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t  </li><li>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a id=\"courses-tab\" href=\"/faculty/directory/10992/Zittrain/courses\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"icon icon-open-book\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"tab-label\">Courses</span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t  </li><li>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a id=\"background-tab\" href=\"/faculty/directory/10992/Zittrain/background\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"icon icon-profile\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"tab-label\">Background</span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t  </li><li>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a id=\"additional-tab\" href=\"/faculty/directory/10992/Zittrain/additional\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"icon icon-briefcase\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"tab-label\">More Info</span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t  </li></ul></div>\n          \t\t\t\t<div id=\"about-panel\" class=\"active-panel content-panel\"><h2>Biography</h2><p></p><p>Jonathan Zittrain is the George Bemis Professor of International Law at Harvard Law School. He is also a&nbsp; professor at the Harvard Kennedy School of Government, a professor of computer science at the Harvard School of Engineering and Applied Sciences, director of the Harvard Law School Library, and co-founder and director of Harvard\u2019s&nbsp;<a href=\"https://cyber.harvard.edu/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Berkman Klein Center for Internet &amp; Society</a>.</p>\n<p>His research interests include the ethics and governance of artificial intelligence; battles for control of digital property; the regulation of cryptography; new privacy frameworks for loyalty to users of online services; the roles of intermediaries within Internet architecture; and the useful and unobtrusive deployment of technology in education.</p>\n<p>Zittrain established the <a href=\"https://www.bkmla.org/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Assembly Program</a>, a three-track fellowship program that convenes cohorts of experts, professionals, and students to develop solutions to complex technology policy issues, including those in cybersecurity, AI, and online disinformation. He also championed the development of the <a href=\"https://case.law/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Caselaw Access Project</a>, which has expanded free public access to U.S. case law.</p>\n<p>Zittrain is a member of the American Academy of Arts and Sciences and the Board of Directors of the Electronic Frontier Foundation. He has served on the Board of Advisors for Scientific American, as a Trustee of the Internet Society, and as a Forum Fellow of the World Economic Forum, which named him a Young Global Leader. He was the Distinguished Scholar-in-Residence at the Federal Communications Commission, where he chaired the Open Internet Advisory Committee. His book, <a href=\"https://yalebooks.yale.edu/book/9780300124873/future-internet-and-how-stop-it\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"><em>The Future of the Internet - And How to Stop It</em></a>, predicted the end of general purpose client computing and the corresponding rise of new gatekeepers. It is available from Yale University Press and Penguin UK, and under a Creative Commons license. That and other works may be found at &lt;<a href=\"http://www.jz.org\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">jz.org</a>&gt;.</p>\n<p></p><p></p><h2>Areas of Interest</h2><ul class=\"links\"><li>Intellectual Property: Control of Digital Property and Content</li><li>Cyber Law and Policy and the Internet: Cyberlaw</li><li>Cyber Law and Policy and the Internet: Electronic Commerce</li><li>Cyber Law and Policy and the Internet: Internet Governance</li><li>Intellectual Property</li><li>Privacy Law</li><li>Tort Law and Theory</li><li>National Security Law</li></ul></div>\n          \t\t\t\t<div id=\"adv-search-link\" style=\"display: none;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/faculty/bibliography/?resourceID=10992\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<i class=\"fa fa-search\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span id=\"adv-search-text\">Advanced Search scholarship by <span id=\"instructor-link\">Jonathan L. class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</span></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">This report from the Berkman Center's Berklett Cybersecurity Project offers a new perspective on the \"going dark\" debate from the discussion, debate, and analyses of an unprecedentedly diverse group of security and policy experts from academia, civil society, and the U.S. intelligence community.\n\nThe Berklett group took up some of the questions of surveillance and encryption as some companies are encrypting services by default, making their customers' messages accessible only to the customers themselves. The report outlines how market forces and commercial interest as the increasing prevalence of networked sensors in machines and appliances point to a future with more opportunities for surveillance, not less.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-87322\" class=\"entry favorite\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"bookmark\" title=\"Representative Publication\"><i class=\"fa fa-bookmark\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan L. Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Engineering an Election</span>, 127 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Harv. L. Rev. F</span>. 335 (2014).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Government &amp; Politics</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Elections &amp; Voting</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://cdn.harvardlawreview.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/06/vol127_Symposium_Zittrain.pdf\"><i title=\"PDF\" class=\"fa fa-file-pdf-o\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</span></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-83287\" class=\"entry favorite\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"bookmark\" title=\"Representative Publication\"><i class=\"fa fa-bookmark\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\"><span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Jonathan L. Zittrain, The Future of the Internet -- And How to Stop It</span> (Yale Univ. Press &amp; Penguin UK 2008).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Communications Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://futureoftheinternet.org/\"><i title=\"futureoftheinternet.org\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-book type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Book</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</span></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">This extraordinary book explains the engine that has catapulted the Internet from backwater to ubiquity\u2014and reveals that it is sputtering precisely because of its runaway success. With the unwitting help of its users, the generative Internet is on a path to a lockdown, ending its cycle of innovation\u2014and facilitating unsettling new kinds of control.\n\nIPods, iPhones, Xboxes, and TiVos represent the first wave of Internet-centered products that can\u2019t be easily modified by anyone except their vendors or selected partners. These \u201ctethered appliances\u201d have already been used in remarkable but little-known ways: car GPS systems have been reconfigured at the demand of law enforcement to eavesdrop on the occupants at all times, and digital video recorders have been ordered to self-destruct thanks to a lawsuit against the manufacturer thousands of miles away. New Web 2.0 platforms like Google mash-ups and Facebook are rightly touted\u2014but their applications can be similarly monitored and eliminated from a central source. As tethered appliances and applications eclipse the PC, the very nature of the Internet\u2014its \u201cgenerativity,\u201d or innovative character\u2014is at risk.\n\nThe Internet\u2019s current trajectory is one of lost opportunity. Its salvation, Zittrain argues, lies in the hands of its millions of users. Drawing on generative technologies like Wikipedia that have so far survived their own successes, this book shows how to develop new technologies and social structures that allow users to work creatively and collaboratively, participate in solutions, and become true \u201cnetizens.\u201d</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-134676\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">A World Without Privacy Will Revive the Masquerade</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Atlantic</span> (Feb. 7, 2020).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.theatlantic.com/technology/archive/2020/02/we-may-have-no-privacy-things-can-always-get-worse/606250/\"><i title=\"www.theatlantic.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-133095\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">John Bowers &amp; Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Answering Impossible Questions: Content Governance in an Age of Disinformation</span>, 1 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Harv. Misinformation Rev</span>. 1 (2020).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Communications Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://doi.org/10.37016/mr-2020-005\"><span class=\"link-icon doi-logo\" title=\"DOI\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In . Rights-era conversations and initiatives amongst regulators and the public at large centered dominantly on protecting nascent spaces for online discourse against external coercion. The values and doctrine developed in the Rights era have been vigorously contested in the Public Health era, during which regulators and advocates have focused (with minimal success) on establishing accountability for concrete harms arising from online content, even where addressing those harms would mean limiting speech. In the era of Process, platforms, regulators, and users must transcend this stalemate between competing values frameworks, not necessarily by uprooting Rights-era cornerstones like CDA 230, but rather by working towards platform governance processes capable of building broad consensus around how policy decisions are made and implemented. Some promising steps in this direction could include delegating certain key policymaking decisions to entities outside of the platforms themselves; making platforms \u201cinformation\u201d or \u201ccontent\u201d fiduciaries; and systematically archiving data and metadata about disinformation detected and addressed by platforms.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-131742\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">A Jury of Random People Can Do Wonders for Facebook</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">The Atlantic</span> (Nov. 14, 2019, 6:00 AM).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Government &amp; Politics</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Elections &amp; M164.6,139.2V76.1H185v63.1H164.6z\"></path>\n\t</g>\n</svg>\n</span></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">To understand where digital governance is going, we must take stock of where it\u2019s been, because the timbre of mainstream thinking around digital governance today is dramatically different than it was when study of \u201cInternet governance\u201d coalesced in the late 1990s.\n\nPerhaps the most obvious change has been from emphasizing networked technologies\u2019 positive effects and promise \u2013 couched around concepts like connectivity, innovation, and, by this author, \u201cgenerativity\u201d \u2013 to pointing out their harms and threats. It\u2019s not that threats weren\u2019t previously recognized, but rather that they were more often seen in external clamps on technological development and upon the corresponding new freedoms for users, whether government intervention to block VOIP services like Skype to protect incumbent telco revenues, or in the shaping of technology to effect undue surveillance, whether for government or corporate purposes.\n\nThe shift in emphasis from positive to negative corresponds to a change in the overarching frameworks for talking about regulating information technology. We have moved from a discourse around rights \u2013 particularly those of end-users, and the ways in which abstention by intermediaries is important to facilitate citizen flourishing \u2013 to one of public health, which naturally asks for a weighing of the systemic benefits or harms of a technology, and to think about what systemic interventions might curtail its apparent excesses.\n\nEach framework captures important values around the use of technology that can both empower and limit individual freedom of action, including to engage in harmful conduct. Our goal today should be to identify where competing values frameworks themselves preclude understanding of others\u2019 positions about regulation, and to see if we can map a path forward that, if not reconciling the frameworks, allows for satisfying, if ever-evolving, resolutions to immediate questions of public and private governance.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-131144\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">The Hidden Costs of Automated Thinking</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">NewYorker.com</span> (July 23, 2019).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Medical Technology</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.newyorker.com/tech/annals-of-technology/the-hidden-costs-of-automated-thinking\"><i title=\"www.newyorker.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122548\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Samuel G. Finlayson, John D. Bowers, Joichi Ito, Jonathan L. Zittrain, Andrew L. Beam &amp; Isaac S. Kohane, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Adversarial Attacks on Medical Machine Learning</span>, 363 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Science</span> 1287 (2019).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Health Care</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Health Law &amp; Policy</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Medical Technology</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://doi.org/10.1126/science.aaw4399\"><span class=\"link-icon doi-logo\" title=\"DOI\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In . These vulnerabilities allow a small, carefully designed change in how inputs are presented to a system to completely alter its output, causing it to confidently arrive at manifestly wrong conclusions. These advanced techniques to subvert otherwise-reliable machine-learning systems\u2014so-called adversarial attacks\u2014have, to date, been of interest primarily to computer science researchers (1). However, the landscape of often-competing interests within health care, and billions of dollars at stake in systems' outputs, implies considerable problems. We outline motivations that various players in the health care system may have to use adversarial attacks and begin a discussion of what to do about them. Far from discouraging continued innovation with medical machine learning, we call for active engagement of medical, technical, legal, and ethical experts in pursuit of efficient, broadly available, and effective health care that machine learning will enable.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-131233\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan L. Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">John Perry Barlow\u2019s Call For Persuasion Over Power</span>, 18 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Duke L. &amp; Tech. Rev</span>. 137 (2019).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Property Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Intellectual Property - Copyright</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Intellectual Property Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Digital Property</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cooperation, Peer-Production &amp; Sharing</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://scholarship.law.duke.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1347&amp;context=dltr\"><i title=\"scholarship.law.duke.edu\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">John Perry Barlow's insights were inseparable from his lyrical way of conveying them. Barlow's expression mates joy and canniness, and one of his talents in writing about new technologies was to flip our conception of the status quo in order to correct it. In 1994, the conventional sense was that the Internet and its champions were heedlessly upsetting a longstanding set of relationships and legal entitlements, with copyright as a signal example. And while that was superficially true, it wasn't the whole story. This brief essay examines the legacy of Barlow's work from the vantage point of today's markedly different digital world.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-109708\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">The Internet</span>, <span class=\"italics-citation\">in</span> <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">A History of Intellectual Property in 50 Objects</span> 369 (Dan Hunter &amp; Claudy Op Den Camp eds., 2019).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Intellectual Property Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Communications Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://ssrn.com/abstract=3373352\"><span class=\"link-icon ssrn-logo\" title=\"SSRN\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In . class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">In a book chartered to demonstrate intellectual property in objects, what concrete thing can represent the Internet, a phenomenon that exists only as a well-elaborated idea? Perhaps the best physical representation of the genius of the Internet\u2014and in particular, \u201cInternet Protocol\u201d\u2014is found in an hourglass.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122273\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Fixing the Internet</span>, 362 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Science</span> 871 (2018).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://doi.org/10.1126/science.aaw0798\"><span class=\"link-icon doi-logo\" title=\"DOI\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In . Concern over the problem is global. However, much remains unknown regarding the vulnerabilities of individuals, institutions, and society to manipulations by malicious actors. A new system of safeguards is needed. Below, we discuss extant social and computer science research regarding belief in fake news and the mechanisms by which it spreads. Fake news has a long history, but we focus on unanswered scientific questions raised by the proliferation of its most recent, politically oriented incarnation. Beyond selected references in the text, suggested further reading can be found in the supplementary materials.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-109316\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Samantha Bates, John Bowers, Shane Greenstein, Jordi Weinstock, Yunhan Xu &amp; Jonathan L. Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Evidence of Decreasing Internet Entropy: The Lack of Redundancy in DNS Resolution by Major Websites and Services</span> (NBER Working Paper No. w24317, Feb. 21, 2018).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Communications Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://www.nber.org/papers/w24317.pdf\"><i title=\"PDF\" class=\"fa fa-file-pdf-o\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">This paper analyzes the extent to which the Internet\u2019s global domain name resolution (DNS) system has preserved its distributed resilience given the rise of cloud-based hosting and infrastructure. We explore trends in the concentration of the DNS space since at least 2011. In addition, we examine changes in domains\u2019 tendency to \u201cdiversify\u201d their pool of nameservers \u2013 how frequently domains employ DNS management services from multiple providers rather than just one provider \u2013 a comparatively costless and therefore puzzlingly rare decision that could supply redundancy and resilience in the event of an attack or service outage affecting one provider.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-109065\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Chelsea Barabas, Karthik Dinakar, Joichi Ito, Madars Virza &amp; Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Interventions Over Predictions: Reframing the Ethical Debate for Actuarial Risk Assessment</span>, 81 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Proc. Machine Learning Res</span>. 62 (2018).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Criminal Law &amp; Procedure</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Legal Profession</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Criminal Justice &amp; Law Enforcement</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Legal Ethics</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://proceedings.mlr.press/v81/barabas18a/barabas18a.pdf\"><i title=\"PDF\" class=\"fa fa-file-pdf-o\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">Actuarial risk assessments might be unduly perceived as a neutral way to counteract implicit bias and increase the fairness of decisions made at almost every juncture of the criminal justice system, from pretrial release to sentencing, parole and probation. In recent times these assessments have come under increased scrutiny, as critics claim that the statistical techniques underlying them might reproduce existing patterns of discrimination and historical biases that are reflected in the data. Much of this debate is centered around competing notions of fairness and predictive accuracy, resting on the contested use of variables that act as \"proxies\" for characteristics legally protected against discrimination, such as race and gender. We argue that a core ethical debate surrounding the use of regression in risk assessments is not simply one of bias or accuracy. Rather, it's one of purpose. If machine learning is operationalized merely in the service of predicting individual future crime, then it becomes difficult to break cycles of criminalization that are driven by the iatrogenic effects of the criminal justice system itself. We posit that machine learning should not be used for prediction, but rather to surface covariates that are fed into a causal model for understanding the social, structural and psychological drivers of crime. We propose an alternative application of machine learning and causal inference away from predicting risk scores to risk mitigation.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-109596\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\"><span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Jonathan Zittrain</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Torts!</span> (2nd ed. 2017).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Civil Practice &amp; Procedure</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Legal Profession</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Torts</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Legal Education</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-book type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Book</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">What\u2019s a tort? It\u2019s a wrong that a court is prepared to recognize, usually in the form of ordering the transfer of money (\u201cdamages\u201d) from the wrongdoer to the wronged. The court is usually alerted to wrong by the filing of a lawsuit: anyone can walk through the courthouse doors and, subject to the limits explored in civil procedure, call someone else (or, if a company, some-thing) to account. We\u2019ll discuss the sources that courts turn to in order to answer such questions. Rarely, in tort cases, are those sources the ones laypeople expect: statutes passed by legislatures. Without statutes to guide them, what are courts left with?</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-109707\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">CDA 230 Then and Now: Does Intermediary Immunity Keep the Rest of Us Healthy?</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">The Recorder</span> (Nov. 10, 2017, 1:45 AM).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Communications Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.law.com/therecorder/sites/therecorder/2017/11/10/cda-230-then-and-now-does-intermediary-immunity-keep-the-rest-of-us-healthy/\"><i title=\"www.law.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-100289\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">\u201cNetwar\u201d: The Unwelcome Militarization of the Internet Has Arrived</span>, 73 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Bull. That made talk of \u201ccyberwar\u201d exaggerated, except in very limited instances. Today that is no longer true: States and their militaries see the value not only of controlling networks for surveillance or to deny access to adversaries, but also of subtle propaganda campaigns launched through a small number of wildly popular worldwide platforms such as Facebook and Twitter. This form of hybrid conflict \u2013 launched by states without state insignia, on privately built and publicly used services \u2013 offers a genuine challenge to those who steward the network and the private companies whose platforms are targeted. While interventions by one state may be tempered by defense by another state, there remain novel problems to solve when what users see and learn online is framed as organic and user-generated but in fact it is not.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122550\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Mass Hacks of Private Email Aren\u2019t Whistleblowing, They are at Odds With It</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Just Security</span> (Oct. 19, 2016).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.justsecurity.org/33677/mass-hacks-private-email-arent-whistleblowing-odds-it/\"><i title=\"www.justsecurity.org\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">The world of 2016 is one where leaking a lot is much easier than leaking a little. And the indiscriminate compromise of people\u2019s selfies, ephemeral data, and personal correspondence \u2014 what we used to rightly think of as a simple and brutal invasion of privacy \u2014 has become the unremarkable chaff surrounding a few worthy instances of potentially genuine whistleblowing.  These now-routine Exxon Valdez spill-sized leaks, for which anyone can be a target, threaten us as individuals and as a citizenry.  They\u2019re not at all like the Pentagon Papers or the revelations of Watergate, and they wrongly benefit from the general feeling that such leaks are a way to bring powerful parties to account.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122549\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jack M. Balkin &amp; Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">A Grand Bargain to Make Tech Companies Trustworthy</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Atlantic</span> (Oct. 3, 2016).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Banking &amp; Finance</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Consumer Finance</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Fiduciary Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Consumer Protection Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.theatlantic.com/technology/archive/2016/10/information-fiduciary/502346/\"><i title=\"www.theatlantic.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">Doctors and lawyers are prohibited from using clients\u2019 information for their own interests, so why aren\u2019t Google and Facebook?</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122570\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Apple's Emoji Gun Control</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">N.Y. class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">June 2014 saw a media uproar about Facebook's emotional contagion study, published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences. In conjunction with researchers at Cornell, Facebook designed an experiment which altered the Facebook News Feed to explore if emotions can spread through Facebook. These feeds, the primary activity and content list on Facebook, are populated according to a proprietary algorithm. In the experiment, the algorithms for a random subset of users were manipulated to display either proportionately more negative emotional content or proportionately more emotional content; a control group saw content according to the current algorithm.\n\nThis study met vocal opposition not solely for manipulating the moods of Facebook users, but also because users neither volunteered nor opted in to such research, and were not informed of their participation in the study. This study is a motivating example of the moral, legal, and technical questions raised when algorithms permeate society.\n\nThis case explains the parameters of the experiment, the reaction in the media, and the legal issues introduced (including Federal Trade Commission standards for commercial practices and the US Department of Health and Human Services Policy for the Protection of Human Research Subjects, informally known as the \"Common Rule\"). In order to encourage examining the issues presented by algorithms in a number of different scenarios, the case uses six hypothetical situations that encourage participants to ponder the use of algorithms in different mediums, including their use with print media, charity, and business, among others. These hypothetical scenarios present varying aspects of the expanding role algorithms play, and will elicit more meaningful discussion and force participants to address through nuanced arguments the complicated issues surrounding algorithms. After briefing analyzing and debating all six scenarios, participants will delve deeper into one hypothetical, using their position on a hypothetical issue to inform their stance on the Facebook Emotional Contagion study. The exercise concludes with a class-wide debate of the ethics surrounding the Facebook Emotional Contagion study.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122554\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">The Future of the Internet: Balancing Security With Openness in the Internet of Things</span>, <span class=\"italics-citation\">in</span> <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Future of Quality Report</span> (ASQ, 2015).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://nrs.harvard.edu/urn-3:HUL.InstRepos:34961942\"><span class=\"link-icon harvard-logo\" title=\"Harvard\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In . class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">This publication is the second annual report of the Internet Monitor project at the Berkman Centerfor Internet &amp; Society at Harvard University. As with the inaugural report, this year\u2019s edition is a collaborative effort of the extended Berkman community. Internet Monitor 2014: Reflections on the Digital World includes nearly three dozen contributions from friends and colleagues around the world that highlight and discuss some of the most compelling events and trends in the digitally networked environment over the past year.\n\nThe result, intended for a general interest audience, brings together reflection and analysis on a broad range of issues and regions \u2014 from an examination of Europe\u2019s \u201cright to be forgotten\u201d to a review of the current state of mobile security to an exploration of a new wave of movements attempting to counter hate speech online \u2014 and offers it up for debate and discussion. Our goal remains not to provide a definitive assessment of the \u201cstate of the Internet\u201d but rather to provide a rich compendium of commentary on the year\u2019s developments with respect to the online space.\n\nLast year\u2019s report examined the dynamics of Internet controls and online activity through the actions of government, corporations, and civil society. We focus this year on the interplay between technological platforms and policy; growing tensions between protecting personal privacy and using big data for social good; the implications of digital communications tools for public discourse and collective action; and current debates around the future of Internet governance.\n\nThe report reflects the diversity of ideas and input the Internet Monitor project seeks to invite. Some of the contributions are descriptive; others prescriptive. Some contain purely factual observations; others offer personal opinion. In addition to those in traditional essay format, contributions this year include a speculative fiction story exploring what our increasingly data-driven world might bring, a selection of \u201cvisual thinking\u201d illustrations that accompany a number of essays, a \u201cYear in Review\u201d timeline that highlights many of the year\u2019s most fascinating Internet-related news stories (and an interactive version of which is available at the netmonitor.org), and a slightly tongue-in-cheek \u201cBy the Numbers\u201d section that offers a look at the year\u2019s important digital statistics. We believe that each contribution offers insights, and hope they provoke further reflection, conversation, and debate in both offline and online settings around the globe.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-16661\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan L. Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Reflections on Internet Culture</span>, 13 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">J. Visual Culture</span> 388 (2014).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://doi.org/10.1177/1470412914544540\"><span class=\"link-icon doi-logo\" title=\"DOI\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In . The distributed environment of the internet allows memes to morph and become distanced from their original intentions. As meme culture becomes more and more assimilated into popular culture, subcultures like those of Reddit or 4chan have begun to re-conceptualize their own role from just meme propagators to cultural producers. Memes can gain commercial appeal, much to the chagrin of their creators. More strangely, memes can gain political traction and affiliation, like American conservative commentator Bill O\u2019Reilly\u2019s \u2018You can\u2018t explain that\u2019 or Anonymous\u2019 \u2018Low Orbit Ion Cannon\u2019. Can meme culture survive becoming not just the property of geeks and nerds, but part of the commercial and political world?</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-83294\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan L. Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">The Case for Kill Switches in Military Weaponry</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Sci. Am</span>., Nov. 2014, at 14.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/the-case-for-kill-switches-in-military-weaponry/\"><i title=\"www.scientificamerican.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">In this article the author discusses aspects of smart technology that might have disarmed the insurgent group in Iraq, the Islamic State (ISIS), without bombs or bullets. Topics include remotely disabling weaponry with a kill switch similar to one on the iPhone, which resulted in reduced iPhone thefts, examples of fail-safe mechanisms that could be built using basic signature-and-authentication technologies, and the implications of a failed kill-switch strategy.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-83293\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan L. This form of reference rot, commonly referred to as \u2018linkrot\u2019, has arisen from the disconnect between the transience of online materials and the permanence of legal citation, and will only become more prevalent as scholarly materials move online. The present paper, written by Jonathan Zittrain, Kendra Albert and Lawrence Lessig, explores the pervasiveness of linkrot in academic and legal citations, finding that more than 70% of the URLs within the Harvard Law Review and other journals, and 50% of the URLs within United States Supreme Court opinions, do not link to the originally cited information. In light of these results, a solution is proposed for authors and editors of new scholarship that involves libraries undertaking the distributed, long-term preservation of link contents.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-88492\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, Opinion, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Don\u2019t Force Google to \u2018Forget\u2019</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">N.Y. What makes it work? And is it dying? This paper is drawn from a talk delivered by Prof. Zittrain to the Royal Society Discussion Meeting 'Web science: a new frontier' in September 2010. It covers key questions about the way the Web works, and how an understanding of its past can help those theorizing about the future. The original Web allowed users to display and send information from their individual computers, and organized the resources of the Internet with uniform resource locators. In the 20 years since then, the Web has evolved. These new challenges require a return to the spirit of the early Web, exploiting the power of the Web's users and its distributed nature to overcome the commercial and geopolitical forces at play. The future of the Web rests in projects that preserve its spirit, and in the Web science that helps make them possible.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-87333\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Alicia Solow-Niederman, Kevin Tsai, Andrew G. class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">This briefing document was developed as part of a March 30, 2012 workshop entitled \u201cPublic Networks for Public Safety: A Workshop on the Present and Future of Mesh Networking,\u201d hosted by the Berkman Center for Internet &amp; Society at Harvard University. The event provided a starting point for conversation about whether mesh networks could be adopted within consumer technologies to enhance public safety communications and empower and connect the public while simultaneously improving public safety. Participants in this initial convening included members of government agencies, academia, the telecommunications industry, and civil society organizations; their helpful inputs were integral to the final version of this document.\n\nBuilding on the dialogue at this gathering, this briefing document seeks to: sketch a broad overview of mobile ad hoc networks (MANETs) and mesh technologies; identify critical technical issues and questions regarding the communications effectiveness of those technologies; explain how public safety communications relate to mesh and offer a synopsis of current regulations affecting those communications; describe a set of basic use cases that emerged from the conference; map out stakeholders at the technical, regulatory, legal, and social levels, and associated interests, points of connection, and potential challenges; catalog select examples and, where possible, highlight potential next steps and areas for short term action; and, summarize key takeaways from the conference, with an emphasis on shared principles or best practices that might inform participants\u2019 diverse efforts to improve communications affordances for the public and the public safety community.\n\nThe paper also synthesizes several strains of workshop discussion that probed big picture framing concerns that could inform the present and future of mesh. Specifically, it puts forth two related but distinct models for mesh: mesh in a technical sense and mesh as a metaphor or social layer construct, with a particular emphasis on the need for further conceptual development with regard to \u201csocial mesh.\u201d The final section emphasizes key take-aways from the event, highlighting core principles and best practices that might both provide a theoretical underpinning for the future conceptual development of mesh networking technologies and social mesh models, respectively, and inform the real-world development of communications systems that involve either definition of mesh.\n\nThe Berkman Center thanks all of the workshop attendees both for their participation during the event and for comments offered during the development of this briefing document. Berkman Center Project Coordinator Alicia Solow-Niederman worked closely with Professor Jonathan Zittrain to plan and execute this event as well as to produce this briefing document. Berkman Center Research Assistants Andrew Crocker and Kevin Tsai provided exceptional research and contributions to this briefing document, and June Casey contributed indispensable support with background research.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122557\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">John Palfrey &amp; Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Better Data for a Better Internet</span>, 334 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Science</span> 1210 (2011).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Communications Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://doi.org/10.1126/science.1210737\"><span class=\"link-icon doi-logo\" title=\"DOI\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In . The resulting debate complemented speculation on the effects of social media in the Arab Spring and the widespread critique of President Mubarak's decision to shut off the Internet and mobile phone systems completely in Egypt (see the photo).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122556\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Did Steve Jobs Favor or Oppose Internet Freedom?</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Sci. Am.</span> (Dec. 1, 2011).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Consumer Finance</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Consumer Protection Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/freedom-fighter/\"><i title=\"www.scientificamerican.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">In 1977, 22-year-old Steve Jobs introduced the world to one of the first self-contained personal computers, the Apple II. The machine was a bold departure from previous products built to perform specific tasks: turn it on, and there was only a blinking cursor awaiting further instruction. Some owners were inspired to program the machines themselves, but others could load up software written and shared or sold by others more skilled or inspired.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122558\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Jonathan Zittrain Responds to Duncan Hollis</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Opinio Juris</span> (July 13, 2011).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Government &amp; Politics</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">National Security Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://opiniojuris.org/2011/07/13/hilj-zittrain-responds-to-hollis/\"><i title=\"opiniojuris.org\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122572\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Encourage More All-Night Hackathons</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">N.Y. class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-gavel type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Other</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">In August 2010, selected faculty and researchers at the Berkman Center for Internet &amp; Society at Harvard University, an independent, exploratory study analyzing ICANN\u2019s decision-making processes and communications with its stakeholders. The study focused on developing a framework and recommendations for understanding and improving ICANN\u2019s accountability and transparency.\n\nThe study was undertaken as part of ICANN\u2019s first Accountability and Transparency Review. On November 4, 2010, the Berkman team\u2019s independent report was publicly posted alongside ICANN\u2019s Accountability and Transparency Review Team's Draft Proposed Recommendations for Public Comment.\n\nThe Executive Summary below outlines key Findings and Recommendations for Improvement. In addition to this Final Report, associated research materials, resources, and other supplementary inputs that were gathered in the course of the Berkman team\u2019s work.\n\n1. Problem Statement:\nIn recent years, ICANN has taken important actions \u2014 ranging from significant policy changes to formal reviews \u2014 to improve its accountability, transparency, and the quality of its decision making. Despite considerable efforts and acknowledged improvements, ICANN continues to struggle with making decisions that the global Internet community can support.\n\n2. Independent Review of Transparency and Accountability at ICANN:\nAs part of a larger independent review process, faculty and researchers from the Berkman Center for Internet &amp; Society have taken on the challenge of researching ICANN\u2019s current efforts to improve accountability via mechanisms of transparency, public participation and corporate governance, and of analyzing key problems and issues across these areas.\n\n3. Findings and Assessment:\nIn-depth research into the three focus areas of this report reveals a highly complex picture with many interacting variables that make fact-finding challenging and also render simple solutions impossible. With this complexity in mind, and referring to the main text of the report for a more granular analysis, the findings and assessments of this report can be condensed as follows.\n\nICANN\u2019s performance regarding transparency is currently not meeting its potential across all areas reviewed and shows deficits along a number of dimensions. It calls for clearly defined improvements at the level of policy, information design, and decision making.\n\nICANN has made significant progress in improving its public participation mechanisms and gets high marks regarding its overall trajectory in this regard. Remaining concerns about the practical impact of public participation on Board decisions are best addressed by increasing visibility and traceability of individual inputs, in order to clarify how these inputs ultimately factor into ICANN decision-making processes.\n\nICANN\u2019s greatest challenge ahead, despite significant recent efforts, remains corporate and Board governance. Proposed measures identified in this report aim to increase efficiency, transparency and accountability within the current context and in the absence of standard accountability mechanisms.\n\n4. Recommendations:\nThere is no straightforward way to address the various challenges ICANN faces. The approach underlying this report\u2019s recommendations takes an evolutionary rather than revolutionary perspective. This approach is aimed at continually improving ICANN\u2019s accountability step by step, based on lessons learned, through a series of measured interventions, reinforced by monitoring and subsequent re-evaluation.\n\nFor each of the three focal areas covered in this report and for each of the key issues addressed, this report suggests ways in which the status quo can be improved. Some of these recommendations can be implemented quickly, others require policy changes, and still others call for more in-depth research, consultation and deliberation among the involved stakeholders.\n\nThis report\u2019s recommendations vary in kind and orientation. They encourage the adoption of best practices where available and experimentation with approaches and tools where feasible. Several of the recommendations are aimed at improving information processing, creation, distribution, and responsiveness at different levels of the organization.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122562\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">The Internet and Press Freedom</span>, 45 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Harv. C.R.-C.L. L. Rev.</span> 563 (2010).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Constitutional Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">First Amendment</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Communications Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://harvardcrcl.org/wp-content/uploads/sites/10/2009/06/563-5761.pdf\"><i title=\"PDF\" class=\"fa fa-file-pdf-o\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">This essay focuses instead on the scripts in which the authors are trapped, and then on a very different kind of fear than that of government encroachment. Many professional journalists of good will and undisputed talent have drifted to a place where they are routinely parties to the absurd and prisoners to threats not as readily grasped as those from official censors.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-87324\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">The Fourth Quadrant</span>, 78 <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Fordham L. Rev</span>. 2767 (2010).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Government &amp; Politics</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">National Security Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Administrative Law &amp; Agencies</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Information Privacy &amp; Security</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"http://ir.lawnet.fordham.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=4542&amp;context=flr\"><i title=\"ir.lawnet.fordham.edu\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">This essay makes the case that Internet-based platforms can be evaluated along two dimensions: the first between generative and sterile, indicating openness to further contribution and development from outsiders, and the second between hierarchy and polyarchy, indicating the ease with which those affected by the platform can escape its umbrella.\n\nThe quadrants formed by these two dimensions can help us to understand patterns in the development of new technologies and platforms, and to brainstorm the widest array of solutions to problems arising under them, particularly problems involving enforcement of regulations against bad actors. Using cybersecurity as a principal example, the essay explains why current national security approaches to Internet vulnerabilities are unduly narrow, and how focusing attention on the \"fourth quadrant\" can broaden the range of options.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122583\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">A Fight Over Freedom at Apple's Core</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Fin. Times</span>, Feb. 4, 2010, at 9.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Science &amp; Technology</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://www.ft.com/content/fcabc720-10fb-11df-9a9e-00144feab49a?mhq5j=e2\"><i title=\"www.ft.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-newspaper-o type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>News</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-87334\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Net Neutrality as Diplomacy</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Yale L. &amp; Pol\u2019y Rev. Inter Alia</span> (May 1, 2010, 10:00 AM).</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">International, Foreign &amp; Comparative Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Foreign Relations</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Treaties &amp; International Agreements</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Networked Society</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://ylpr.yale.edu/inter_alia/net-neutrality-diplomacy\"><i title=\"ylpr.yale.edu\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"type-icon-slot\">Type: <i class=\"fa fa-file-text type-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i>Article</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"abstract\"><strong>Abstract</strong><i class=\"fa fa-plus-square-o abstract-icon\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></div><div class=\"abstract-content\">Popular imagination holds that the turf of a state\u2019s foreign embassy is a little patch of its homeland. Enter the American Embassy in Beijing and you are in the United States. Indeed, in many contexts \u2013 such as resistance to search and seizure by a host country\u2019s authorities \u2013 there is an inviolability to diplomatic outposts. These arrangements have been central to diplomacy for decades so that diplomats can perform their work without fear of harassment and coercion.\n\nComplementing a state\u2019s oasis on foreign territory is the ability to get there and back unharried. Diplomats are routinely granted immunity from detention as they travel, and la valise diplomatique \u2013 the diplomatic pouch \u2013 is a packet that cannot be seized, or in most cases even inspected, as it moves about. Each pouch is a link between a country and its outposts dispersed in alien territory around the world.\n\nCitizens and their digital packets deserve much the same treatment as they traverse the global Internet. Just as states expect to conduct their official business on foreign soil without interference, so citizens should be able to lead digitally mediated \u2013 and increasingly distributed \u2013 lives without fear that their links to their online selves can be arbitrarily abridged or surveilled by their Internet Service Providers or any other party. Just as the sanctity of the embassy and la valise diplomatique is vital to the practice of international diplomacy, the ability of our personal bits to travel about the net unhindered is central to the lives we increasingly live online.\n\nThis frame differs from the usual criteria for debating the merits of net neutrality. It does not focus on what makes for more efficient provision of broad-band services to end users. It is unaffected by what sorts of bundling of services by a local ISP might intrigue the ISP\u2019s subscribers. It does not examine the costs and benefits of faraway content providers being asked to bargain for access to that local ISP\u2019s customers. Instead, it recognizes that Internet users establish outposts far and wide, and that a new status quo of distributed selfhood is quickly taking hold.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t</div><div id=\"entry-122566\" class=\"entry\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div style=\"position: relative;\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"bluebook\">Robert Faris &amp; Jonathan Zittrain, <span class=\"italics-citation\">Web Tactics</span>, <span class=\"small-caps-citation\">Index on Censorship</span>, Nov. 1, 2009, at 90.</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"parent-categories\"><strong>Categories:</strong> <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">International, Foreign &amp; Comparative Law</div>, <div class=\"parent-category-link-entry\">Technology &amp; Law</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"child-categories\"><strong>Sub-Categories:</strong> <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Comparative Law</div>, <div class=\"child-category-link-entry\">Cyberlaw</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class=\"link-list\"><strong>Links:</strong>\u2003<a href=\"https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1080/03064220903467208\"><i title=\"journals.sagepub.com\" class=\"fa fa-globe\" aria-hidden=\"true\"></i></a>\u2003<a href=\"http://doi.org/10.1080/03064220903467208\"><span class=\"link-icon doi-logo\" title=\"DOI\"><!--?xml version=\"1.0\" encoding=\"utf-8\"?-->\n<!-- Generator: Adobe Illustrator 21.0.2, SVG Export Plug-In .
